        <title>Genome analyses provide insights into the evolution and adaptation of the eukaryotic Picophytoplankton Mychonastes homosphaera | BMC Genomics | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Picophytoplankton are abundant and can contribute greatly to primary production in eutrophic lakes. Mychonastes species are among the common eukaryotic picophytoplankton in eutrophic lakes. We used third-generation sequencing technology to sequence the whole genome of Mychonastes homosphaera isolated from Lake Chaohu, a eutrophic freshwater lake in China. The 24.23 Mbp nuclear genome of M.homosphaera, harboring 6649 protein-coding genes, is more compact than the genomes of the closely related Sphaeropleales species. This genome streamlining may be caused by a reduction in gene family number, intergenic size and introns. The genome sequence of M.homosphaera reveals the strategies adopted by this organism for environmental adaptation in the eutrophic lake. Analysis of cultures and the protein complement highlight the metabolic flexibility of M.homosphaera, the genome of which encodes genes involved in light harvesting, carbohydrate metabolism, and nitrogen and microelement metabolism, many of which form functional gene clusters. Reconstruction of the bioenergetic metabolic pathways of M.homosphaera, such as the lipid, starch and isoprenoid pathways, reveals characteristics that make this species suitable for biofuel production. The analysis of the whole genome of M. homosphaera provides insights into the genome streamlining, the high lipid yield, the environmental adaptation and phytoplankton evolution."/>

        <meta property="og:url" content="https://bmcgenomics.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6"/>
        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="BMC Genomics"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="Genome analyses provide insights into the evolution and adaptation of the eukaryotic Picophytoplankton Mychonastes homosphaera"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Picophytoplankton are abundant and can contribute greatly to primary production in eutrophic lakes. Mychonastes species are among the common eukaryotic picophytoplankton in eutrophic lakes. We used third-generation sequencing technology to sequence the whole genome of Mychonastes homosphaera isolated from Lake Chaohu, a eutrophic freshwater lake in China. The 24.23 Mbp nuclear genome of M.homosphaera, harboring 6649 protein-coding genes, is more compact than the genomes of the closely related Sphaeropleales species. This genome streamlining may be caused by a reduction in gene family number, intergenic size and introns. The genome sequence of M.homosphaera reveals the strategies adopted by this organism for environmental adaptation in the eutrophic lake. Analysis of cultures and the protein complement highlight the metabolic flexibility of M.homosphaera, the genome of which encodes genes involved in light harvesting, carbohydrate metabolism, and nitrogen and microelement metabolism, many of which form functional gene clusters. Reconstruction of the bioenergetic metabolic pathways of M.homosphaera, such as the lipid, starch and isoprenoid pathways, reveals characteristics that make this species suitable for biofuel production. The analysis of the whole genome of M. homosphaera provides insights into the genome streamlining, the high lipid yield, the environmental adaptation and phytoplankton evolution."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/12864.jpg"/>

                        <h1 class="c-article-title" data-test="article-title" data-article-title="" itemprop="name headline">Genome analyses provide insights into the evolution and adaptation of the eukaryotic Picophytoplankton <i>Mychonastes homosphaera</i></h1>

    <a data-test="journal-link" href="/"><i data-test="journal-title">BMC Genomics</i></a>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>Picophytoplankton are abundant and can contribute greatly to primary production in eutrophic lakes. <i>Mychonastes</i> species are among the common eukaryotic picophytoplankton in eutrophic lakes. We used third-generation sequencing technology to sequence the whole genome of <i>Mychonastes homosphaera</i> isolated from Lake Chaohu, a eutrophic freshwater lake in China.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Result</h3><p>The 24.23 Mbp nuclear genome of <i>M.homosphaera</i>, harboring 6649 protein-coding genes, is more compact than the genomes of the closely related Sphaeropleales species. This genome streamlining may be caused by a reduction in gene family number, intergenic size and introns. The genome sequence of <i>M.homosphaera</i> reveals the strategies adopted by this organism for environmental adaptation in the eutrophic lake. Analysis of cultures and the protein complement highlight the metabolic flexibility of <i>M.homosphaera</i>, the genome of which encodes genes involved in light harvesting, carbohydrate metabolism, and nitrogen and microelement metabolism, many of which form functional gene clusters. Reconstruction of the bioenergetic metabolic pathways of <i>M.homosphaera</i>, such as the lipid, starch and isoprenoid pathways, reveals characteristics that make this species suitable for biofuel production.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusion</h3><p>The analysis of the whole genome of <i>M. homosphaera</i> provides insights into the genome streamlining, the high lipid yield, the environmental adaptation and phytoplankton evolution.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>As the most urbanized and developed region of China, lake eutrophication is common in the middle-lower reaches of the Yangtze River. Picophytoplankton (with cell diameters &lt; 3 μm) are abundant and can contribute 9–55% of primary productivity in eutrophic lakes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Sieburth JM, Smetacek V, Lenz J. Pelagic ecosystem structure: heterotrophic compartments of the plankton and their relationship to plankton size fractions - comment. Limnol Oceanogr. 1978;23(6):1256–63." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d18118e766">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Li S, Shi X, Lepère C, Liu M, Wang X, Kong F. Unexpected predominance of photosynthetic picoeukaryotes in shallow eutrophic lakes. J Plankton Res. 2016;38(4):830–42." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d18118e769">2</a>]. <i>Mychonastes</i> species are the dominant eukaryotic picophytoplankton in most eutrophic lakes (e.g., Lake Chaohu and Lake Poyang in China) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Li S, Shi X, Lepère C, Liu M, Wang X, Kong F. Unexpected predominance of photosynthetic picoeukaryotes in shallow eutrophic lakes. J Plankton Res. 2016;38(4):830–42." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d18118e775">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Shi X, Li S, Fan F, Zhang M, Yang Z, Yang Y. Mychonastes dominates the photosynthetic picoeukaryotes in Lake Poyang, a river-connected lake. FEMS Microbiol Ecol. 2019;95(1):fiy211." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d18118e778">3</a>]. However, the mechanism underlying the dominance of <i>Mychonastes</i> in eutrophic lakes is not clear. Using a whole-genome approach, we specifically focused on the gene sets and metabolic pathways of <i>Mychonastes</i> that may facilitate its dominance under the environmental conditions of most eutrophic lakes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Gobler CJ, Berry DL, Dyhrman ST, Wilhelm SW, Salamov A, Lobanov AV, Zhang Y, Collier JL, Wurch LL, Kustka AB, et al. Niche of harmful alga Aureococcus anophagefferens revealed through ecogenomics. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2011;108(11):4352–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d18118e788">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Parker MS, Mock T, Armbrust EV. Genomic insights into marine microalgae. Annu Rev Genet. 2008;42:619–45." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d18118e791">5</a>]. Although given the decreasing cost of sequencing [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Schuster SC. Next-generation sequencing transforms today's biology. Nat Methods. 2008;5(1):16–8." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d18118e794">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Metzker ML. Sequencing technologies - the next generation. Nat Rev Genet. 2010;11(1):31–46." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d18118e794_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Goodwin S, McPherson JD, McCombie WR. Coming of age: ten years of next-generation sequencing technologies. Nat Rev Genet. 2016;17(6):333–51." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d18118e797">8</a>], many phytoplankton have been sequenced [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dasgupta CN, Nayaka S, Toppo K, Singh AK, Deshpande U, Mohapatra A. Draft genome sequence and detailed characterization of biofuel production by oleaginous microalga Scenedesmus quadricauda LWG002611. Biotechnol Biofuels. 2018;11:308." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d18118e801">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Suzuki S, Yamaguchi H, Nakajima N, Kawachi M. Raphidocelis subcapitata (=Pseudokirchneriella subcapitata) provides an insight into genome evolution and environmental adaptations in the Sphaeropleales. Sci Rep. 2018;8:8058." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d18118e801_1">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bogen C, Al-Dilaimi A, Albersmeier A, Wichmann J, Grundmann M, Rupp O, Lauersen KJ, Blifernez-Klassen O, Kalinowski J, Goesmann A, et al. Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production. BMC Genomics. 2013;14:926." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d18118e801_2">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Carreres BM, de Jaeger L, Springer J, Barbosa MJ, Breuer G, van den End EJ, Kleinegris DMM, Schaffers I, Wolbert EJH, Zhang H, et al. Draft Genome Sequence of the Oleaginous Green Alga Tetradesmus obliquus UTEX 393. Genome Announc. 2017;5(3):e01449–16." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d18118e804">12</a>], the genome sequencing of picophytoplankton has only targeted marine species thus far [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Derelle E, Ferraz C, Rombauts S, Rouze P, Worden AZ, Robbens S, Partensky F, Degroeve S, Echeynie S, Cooke R, et al. Genome analysis of the smallest free-living eukaryote Ostreococcus tauri unveils many unique features. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2006;103(31):11647–52." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d18118e807">13</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="van Baren MJ, Bachy C, Reistetter EN, Purvine SO, Grimwood J, Sudek S, Yu H, Poirier C, Deerinck TJ, Kuo A, et al. Evidence-based green algal genomics reveals marine diversity and ancestral characteristics of land plants. BMC Genomics. 2016;17:267." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d18118e810">14</a>]. The absence of genome information for picophytoplankton in freshwater lakes prevents us from recognizing the picophytoplankton niche and its ecological role in the lake.</p><p><i>Mychonastes</i> belong to the order Sphaeropleales within the class <i>Chlorophyceae</i>. Sphaeropleales is a large group that contains some of the most common freshwater algae [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wolf M, Buchheim M, Hegewald E, Krienitz L, Hepperle D. Phylogenetic position of the Sphaeropleaceae (Chlorophyta). Plant Syst Evol. 2002;230(3–4):161–71." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d18118e821">15</a>]. The genome sequences of Sphaeropleales are a hot research topic because some of these species show enormous potential for biofuel production [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Suzuki S, Yamaguchi H, Nakajima N, Kawachi M. Raphidocelis subcapitata (=Pseudokirchneriella subcapitata) provides an insight into genome evolution and environmental adaptations in the Sphaeropleales. Sci Rep. 2018;8:8058." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d18118e824">10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Bogen C, Al-Dilaimi A, Albersmeier A, Wichmann J, Grundmann M, Rupp O, Lauersen KJ, Blifernez-Klassen O, Kalinowski J, Goesmann A, et al. Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production. BMC Genomics. 2013;14:926." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d18118e827">11</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Mandal S, Mallick N. Microalga Scenedesmus obliquus as a potential source for biodiesel production. Appl Microbiol Biotechnol. 2009;84(2):281–91." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d18118e830">16</a>], with robust growth and a high lipid content. Thus far, six genomes of Sphaeropleales, belonging to <i>Scenedesmus quadricauda</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Dasgupta CN, Nayaka S, Toppo K, Singh AK, Deshpande U, Mohapatra A. Draft genome sequence and detailed characterization of biofuel production by oleaginous microalga Scenedesmus quadricauda LWG002611. Biotechnol Biofuels. 2018;11:308." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d18118e837">9</a>], <i>Raphidocelis subcapitata</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Suzuki S, Yamaguchi H, Nakajima N, Kawachi M. Raphidocelis subcapitata (=Pseudokirchneriella subcapitata) provides an insight into genome evolution and environmental adaptations in the Sphaeropleales. Sci Rep. 2018;8:8058." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d18118e843">10</a>], <i>Monoraphidium neglectum</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Bogen C, Al-Dilaimi A, Albersmeier A, Wichmann J, Grundmann M, Rupp O, Lauersen KJ, Blifernez-Klassen O, Kalinowski J, Goesmann A, et al. Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production. BMC Genomics. 2013;14:926." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d18118e849">11</a>], <i>Tetradesmus obliquus</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Carreres BM, de Jaeger L, Springer J, Barbosa MJ, Breuer G, van den End EJ, Kleinegris DMM, Schaffers I, Wolbert EJH, Zhang H, et al. Draft Genome Sequence of the Oleaginous Green Alga Tetradesmus obliquus UTEX 393. Genome Announc. 2017;5(3):e01449–16." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d18118e856">12</a>], <i>Chromochloris zofingiensis</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Roth MS, Cokus SJ, Gallaher SD, Walter A, Lopez D, Erickson E, Endelman B, Westcott D, Larabell CA, Merchant SS, et al. Chromosome-level genome assembly and transcriptome of the green alga Chromochloris zofingiensis illuminates astaxanthin production. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2017;114(21):E4296–305." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d18118e862">17</a>], and <i>Coelastrella</i> sp. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Karpagam R, Jawaharraj K, Ashokkumar B, Sridhar J, Varalakshmi P. Unraveling the lipid and pigment biosynthesis in Coelastrella sp M-60: genomics-enabled transcript profiling. Algal Res Biomass Biofuels Bioproducts. 2018;29:277–89." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d18118e868">18</a>], have been sequenced. These Sphaeropleales genomes provide much information for <i>Mychonastes</i> genome research and contribute to explaining the evolution and adaptation of <i>Mychonastes</i>. Comparative analyses of genomes would provide insights into the environmental adaptation and genome evolution of Sphaeropleales.</p><p>In order to further increase knowledge about the evolution and adaptation of freshwater picophytoplankton, we isolated a <i>Mychonastes</i> strain from Lake Chaohu, a highly eutrophic lake, and sequenced its complete genome by using third-generation sequencing (PacBio Sequel). Here, we conducted combined analysis of the complete genome sequences of <i>M.homosphaera</i> and other Sphaeropleales species as well as picophytoplankton species to investigate the evolutionary history and environmental adaptation of <i>M.homosphaera</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Phylogenetic analyses</h3><p>We performed phylogenetic analyses using 18S rRNA to verify the phylogenetic position of <i>M.homosphaera</i> within Viridiplantae, with red algae as an outgroup (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig1">1</a>). In the tree, <i>M.homosphaera</i> was clustered by family, forming a monophyletic group with the other <i>Mychonastaceae</i> species. There was robust support (BP = 95) for the inclusion of <i>M.homosphaera</i> in <i>Mychonastaceae</i>, where it was positioned closest to <i>Mychonastes homosphaera</i> (AB025423) isolated from Lake Kinneret, Israel [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Hanagata N, Malinsky-Rushansky N, Dubinsky Z. Eukaryotic picoplankton, Mychonastes homosphaera (Chlorophyceae, Chlorophyta), in Lake Kinneret, Israel. Phycol Res. 1999;47(4):263–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d18118e924">19</a>].
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="326" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Phylogenetic tree of 18S rDNA sequences using the maximum likelihood method</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">General features of the nuclear genome</h3><p>We sequenced 5.8 Gbp reads using the PacBio Sequel system. Based on assembly and correction, we obtained <i>M.homosphaera</i> genome statistics (genome size: 24.23 Mb, contig N50: 2 Mb, contig number: 31) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Tab1">1</a>). The assembly was analyzed regarding its completeness based on sequence homology to the OrthoDB eukaryote dataset (<a href="http://www.orthodb.org">www.orthodb.org</a>), showing 89.4% complete BUSCOs (Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">1</a>), which was higher than the percentages for the sequenced Sphaeropleales species (<i>C.zofingiensis</i> 84.5%, <i>M.neglectum</i> 58.5%, and <i>T.obliquus</i> 79.9%) except for <i>R.subcapitata</i> (91.7%) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Suzuki S, Yamaguchi H, Nakajima N, Kawachi M. Raphidocelis subcapitata (=Pseudokirchneriella subcapitata) provides an insight into genome evolution and environmental adaptations in the Sphaeropleales. Sci Rep. 2018;8:8058." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d18118e981">10</a>]. Therefore, we obtained a nearly complete genome for <i>M.homosphaera.</i></p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 <i>Mychonastes homosphaera</i> genome statistics</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A total of 53,016 SSRs (simple sequence repeats) were masked by MISA (MIcroSAtellite identification tool), which accounted for 20.13% of the <i>M.homosphaera</i> genome. There were six types of SSR in the <i>M.homosphaera</i> genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">2</a>), and the vast majority of SSR (52,206 repeat sequences) belong to those three types, p1, p2 and p3. Noncoding RNA in the genome was annotated differently; 26 rRNAs (including 6 18S rRNAs, 7 28S rRNAs, 6 5.8S rRNAs, and 7 5S rRNAs), 46 tRNAs and 11 snRNA were annotated. A total of 6649 protein-coding genes were predicted in the genome, with an average transcript length of 2952.98 bp and an average CDS (coding sequence) length of 1569.72 bp. Out of these, 5711 protein-coding genes (85.89% of the predicted genes) were annotated, and coding sequences constituted 43.1% of the genome, with a mean exon length and mean intron length of 323.36 and 358.88 bp, respectively. The protein-coding genes contained 25,628 introns, with a density of 3.85 introns per gene, and 32,277 exons, with a density of 4.85 introns per gene.</p><p>The nuclear genome of <i>M.homosphaera</i> was the smallest among those known for Sphaeropleales, at less than half of the size of the known whole genome sequences from Sphaeropleales. Unlike other Sphaeropleales species, <i>M.homosphaera</i> exhibited small intergenic regions and a high coding rate, which is common in other picophytoplankton (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig2">2</a>); therefore, the coding percentage of <i>M.homosphaera</i> (43.1%) was higher than that of other Sphaeropleales (expect <i>R.subcapitata</i>). Furthermore, <i>M.homosphaera</i> exhibited the highest GC content (72.4%) among the Sphaeropleales species examined to date.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="222" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Size distributions of nuclear and organellar genomes of <i>M.homosphaera</i>, two Sphaeropleales species (<i>M.neglectum</i> and <i>R.subcapitata</i>) and two picophytoplankton species (<i>O.tauri</i> and <i>M.commoda</i>)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">General features of chloroplast and mitochondrial genomes</h3><p><i>M.homosphaera</i> is the Sphaeropleales picophytoplankton, we compared its organelle genomes with those of other Sphaeropleales species (<i>M.neglectum</i> and <i>R.subcapitata</i>) and those of two marine picophytoplanktons (<i>Ostreococcus tauri</i> and <i>Micromonas commoda</i>), to understand the genome features of <i>M.homosphaera</i>. The complete chloroplast genome of <i>M.homosphaera</i> was one of the smallest among Sphaeropleales species identified thus far (102,771 bp in size, approximately two-thirds the size in other Sphaeropleales species), and it was AT-rich (60.03%) and circular with no inverted repeats or introns (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig2">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig3">3</a>). Surprisingly, <i>M.homosphaera</i> exhibited the maximum number of chloroplast genes among known Sphaeropleales, including 72 conserved protein-coding genes, 6 rRNAs and 35 tRNAs. Intronic ORFs (open reading frames) were not found in the chloroplast genome. Compared with other Sphaeropleales species, <i>M.homosphaera</i> presented extra rpl32 and apoprotein A1 genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">3</a>). However, in fact, the CDS length of <i>M.homosphaera</i> was similar to those of other Sphaeropleales species.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="694" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Chloroplast genome of <i>M.homosphaera</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Extreme gene compaction was also founded in the mitochondrion (25,091 bp, 20.7% GC) (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig2">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig4">4</a>), which presented the smallest mitochondrial genome with the highest protein coding density identified to date within Sphaeropleales species while retaining the same genes found in other species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">4</a>). There were 13 conserved protein-coding genes, 6 fragmented rRNAs, and 22 tRNAs. The protein-coding genes included subunits of NADH dehydrogenase (nad1, nad2, nad3, nad4, nad4L, nad5 and nad6), ubichinol cytochrome c reductase (cob), cytochrome oxidases (cox1, cox2a and cox3) and 2 ATP synthases (atp6 and atp9). Similar with other Sphaeropleales species, the 16S rRNA and 23S rRNA sequences were separated into two and four fragments, respectively. Only a threonine-tRNA gene was missing in the mitochondrial genome, and there was an almost complete set of tRNAs for translation. In addition, we found that Cox2 was split; its N-terminus (Cox2a) was encoded by the mitochondrial genome, and the C-terminus of Cox2 (Cox2b) was encoded by the nuclear genome. Unlike other Sphaeropleales species, there were no introns in the <i>M.homosphaera</i> mitochondrial genome. However, the lack of introns had also been found in the mitochondrial genome of picophytoplankton such as <i>Ostreococcus tauri</i> and <i>Micromonas commoda</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Derelle E, Ferraz C, Rombauts S, Rouze P, Worden AZ, Robbens S, Partensky F, Degroeve S, Echeynie S, Cooke R, et al. Genome analysis of the smallest free-living eukaryote Ostreococcus tauri unveils many unique features. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2006;103(31):11647–52." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d18118e1451">13</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="van Baren MJ, Bachy C, Reistetter EN, Purvine SO, Grimwood J, Sudek S, Yu H, Poirier C, Deerinck TJ, Kuo A, et al. Evidence-based green algal genomics reveals marine diversity and ancestral characteristics of land plants. BMC Genomics. 2016;17:267." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d18118e1454">14</a>] (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig2">2</a>)<i>.</i></p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="745" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Mitochondrial genome of <i>M.homosphaera</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Gene families of the <i>M.homosphaera</i> genus</h3><p>To infer gene families variation in <i>M.homosphaera</i> in evolution, we compared its homologous genes with those of model organism, such as the red algae <i>Cyanidioschyzon merolae</i>, the green plant <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, and two green algae (<i>O.tauri</i> and <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i>). The number of common gene families was 1814, accounting for approximately half of the <i>M.homosphaera</i> gene families (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig5">5</a>). Almost all of <i>M.homosphaera</i> gene families could be found in plants and algae, implying the evolutionarily ancient divergence of Plantae (red algae, green algae, and plants) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Armbrust EV, Berges JA, Bowler C, Green BR, Martinez D, Putnam NH, Zhou SG, Allen AE, Apt KE, Bechner M, et al. The genome of the diatom Thalassiosira pseudonana: ecology, evolution, and metabolism. Science. 2004;306(5693):79–86." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d18118e1519">20</a>]. In accord with the evolutionary direction, 529 gene families were shared by <i>M.homosphaera</i> and the green alga <i>C.reinhardtii</i>, whereas 24 and 5 gene families were only shared by <i>M.homosphaera</i> with <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> and <i>C.merolae</i>, respectively.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="684" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Venn diagram of the gene families of <i>M.homosphaera</i> and other Viridiplantae</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A similar comparison in green algae including Sphaeropleales species (such as <i>M.neglectum</i>, <i>R.subcapitata</i> and <i>M.homosphaera</i>) and <i>C.reinhardtii</i> was also performed (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig6">6</a>). The common numbers of gene families for green algae (<i>M.neglectum</i>, <i>R.subcapitata</i>, <i>M.homosphaera</i> and <i>C.reinhardtii</i>) was 4048, and for Sphaeropleales (<i>M.neglectum</i>, <i>R.subcapitata</i> and <i>M.homosphaera</i>) was 4393. <i>M.homosphaera</i> showed a lack of unique gene families, and more than 90 % of its gene families were common gene families. In addition, comparison of <i>M.homosphaera</i> genes to the nonredundant protein database yielded top hits from a variety of organisms, among which the highest frequency was found for the species <i>M.neglectum</i> and the taxon Chlorophyta (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig7">7</a>), which was expected on the basis of the phylogeny of <i>M.homosphaera.</i></p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig. 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Venn diagram of the gene families of <i>M.homosphaera</i>, two Sphaeropleales species (<i>M.neglectum</i> and <i>R.subcapitata</i>) and a chlorophyte species (<i>C.reinhardtii</i>)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Fig. 7"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="434" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>Top BLASTp hits of <i>M.homosphaera</i> compared with the nonredundant protein database</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genome annotation and insights from the genome</h3><p>The functions of 5711 proteins were predicted in the biochemical pathways of <i>M.homosphaera</i>, among which 3948 proteins were annotated based on homology with proteins in public databases. Furthermore, the annotated proteins were divided into functional categories based on the GO (Gene Ontology) database. The predicted proteins in <i>M.homosphaera</i> genome were divided based on three GO domains: molecular function, cellular component and biological process (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig8">8</a>).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-8" data-title="Fig. 8"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig8" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 8</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/8" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig8_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-8-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig8_HTML.png" alt="figure8" loading="lazy" width="685" height="499" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-8-desc"><p>Gene Ontology (GO) assignments for <i>M.homosphaera</i> The 31 most extensive GO terms of the three GO supercategories “molecular function” (blue), “cellular component” (green) and “biological process” (red) are shown</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/8" data-track-dest="link:Figure8 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Functional analyses using KEGG (Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes) categories showed that most of the functions were shared among phytoplankton, although <i>M.homosphaera</i> possessed the minimum number of genes among phytoplankton families (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig9">9</a>). <i>C.reinhardtii</i>, <i>M.neglectum</i> and <i>M.commoda</i> represent the chlorophyte, Sphaeropleales and picophytoplankton, respectively. However, the number of total genes in <i>M.homosphaera</i> were quite similar to those in other algae. Though the proportion of <i>M.homosphaera</i> genes related to various types of metabolism was relatively small, it possessed genes related to xenobiotic biodegradation, which are lacking in other algae. <i>M.homosphaera</i> contained a higher proportion of genes related to environmental information processing than other algae, especially signal transduction genes. Furthermore, <i>M.homosphaera</i> possessed all cellular process pathway genes, while <i>M.neglectum</i> possess transport, catabolism, cell growth and death pathway genes, and <i>C.reinhardtii</i> and <i>M.commoda</i> only possess transport and catabolism pathway genes.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-9" data-title="Fig. 9"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig9" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 9</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/9" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig9_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-9-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig9_HTML.png" alt="figure9" loading="lazy" width="685" height="470" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-9-desc"><p>Functional comparison of <i>M.homosphaera</i> and other phytoplankton according to KEGG classification. (<b>a</b>), (<b>b</b>), (<b>c</b>) and (<b>d</b>) represent cellular processes, environmental information processing, genetic information processing and metabolism, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/9" data-track-dest="link:Figure9 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">The genes of <i>M.homosphaera</i> facilitate its dominance within the environmental conditions of the lake</h3><p><i>M.homosphaera</i> are widely distributed in shallow turbid lakes and river-connected eutrophic lakes, experiencing complex and changing environmental conditions such as varying water exchange rates, light conditions, temperatures and nutrition concentrations. Genome analysis provides insights into the mechanism for adaptation to these environmental conditions.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec9">Light Harvesting</h4><p><i>M.homosphaera</i> possessed genes homologous to phototropins and cryptochromes, which generally act as blue-light photoreceptors in certain eukaryotes. A phytochrome gene homolog was also identified; phytochromes function as red/far-red light and temperature sensors. However, there were no rhodopsin green-light photoreceptors in <i>M.homosphaera</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig10">10</a>).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-10" data-title="Fig. 10"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig10" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 10</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/10" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig10_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-10-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig10_HTML.png" alt="figure10" loading="lazy" width="685" height="392" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-10-desc"><p>Carbohydrate metabolism, nutrient transport and photoreceptors in <i>Mychonastes homosphaera.</i> The metabolites are shown in black, and the enzymes are shown in yellow. G3P: glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate, MA: malic acid, OAA: oxaloacetate, PEP: phosphoenolpyruvate, 3-PGA: 3-phosphoglycerate, Pyr: pyruvate, RuBP: ribulose-1,5-bisphosphate, BCT: bicarbonate transporter, CA: carbonic anhydrase, MDH: malate dehydrogenase, ME: malic enzyme, PC: pyruvate carboxylase, PEPC: phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase, PPDK: pyruvate, phosphate dikinase, RuBisCO: ribulose-1,5-bisphosphate carboxylase oxygenase, PHO: phototropins, CRY: cryptochromes, PHY: phytochrome. In nutrient transport, a solid line or circle indicates that the gene has been identified, and a dashed circle indicates that the gene may be present. Metabolic pathway reconstruction was performed based on the KEGG database</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/10" data-track-dest="link:Figure10 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The xanthophyll cycle is a major mechanism for the dissipation of excess light energy for plant photoprotection [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Demmig B, Winter K, Kruger A, Czygan FC. Photoinhibition and Zeaxanthin formation in intact leaves -a possible role of the xanthophyll cycle in the dissipation of excess light energy. Plant Physiol. 1987;84(2):218–24." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d18118e1832">21</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Pinnola A, Dall'Osto L, Gerotto C, Morosinotto T, Bassi R, Alboresi A. Zeaxanthin binds to light-harvesting complex stress-related protein to enhance nonphotochemical quenching in Physcomitrella patens. Plant Cell. 2013;25(9):3519–34." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d18118e1835">22</a>]. Two key enzymes of the xanthophyll cycle, VDE (violaxanthin de-epoxidase) and ZE (zeaxanthin epoxidase), can be found in <i>M.homosphaera.</i> In addition, Fe-Mn SOD (superoxide dismutase)-encoding genes were found. Furthermore, the <i>M.homosphaera</i> genome contained the full suite of genes involved in photosynthesis, including 13 genes encoding components of the LHC (light-harvesting complex), which absorbs light and transfers it to photosynthetic reaction centers.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec10">Carbohydrate metabolism</h4><p>Carbon concentrations and carbon assimilation have been described in many algae [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Armbrust EV, Berges JA, Bowler C, Green BR, Martinez D, Putnam NH, Zhou SG, Allen AE, Apt KE, Bechner M, et al. The genome of the diatom Thalassiosira pseudonana: ecology, evolution, and metabolism. Science. 2004;306(5693):79–86." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d18118e1852">20</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Reinfelder JR. Carbon Concentrating Mechanisms in Eukaryotic Marine Phytoplankton. In: Carlson CA, Giovannoni SJ, editors. Annual Review of Marine Science, Vol 3; 2011. p. 291–315." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d18118e1855">23</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Radakovits R, Jinkerson RE, Fuerstenberg SI, Tae H, Settlage RE, Boore JL, Posewitz MC. Draft genome sequence and genetic transformation of the oleaginous alga Nannochloropis gaditana. Nat Commun. 2012;3:10." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d18118e1858">24</a>], and we were able to identify the genes required for both C3 (Calvin cycle)- and C4-type carbon assimilation (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">5</a>). The key enzymes in these two types of metabolism are carbonic anhydrases, which catalyze the reversible conversion of CO<sub>2</sub> to HCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>. In addition, HCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> must be transported into the cell by a bicarbonate transporter or be converted into CO<sub>2</sub> by carbonic anhydrases. These two enzymes were found in <i>M.homosphaera</i> genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig10">10</a>).</p><p>Based on the obtained protein information and the Randor model [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Radakovits R, Jinkerson RE, Fuerstenberg SI, Tae H, Settlage RE, Boore JL, Posewitz MC. Draft genome sequence and genetic transformation of the oleaginous alga Nannochloropis gaditana. Nat Commun. 2012;3:10." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d18118e1884">24</a>], we describe potential carbon-concentrating mechanisms in <i>M.homosphaera</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig10">10</a>). There are two potential C4 carbon-concentrating mechanisms, which involve the production of malate in the cytosol and the mitochondria, and they provide possibilities for carbon concentration under different environmental conditions.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec11">Nitrogen assimilation</h4><p>Nitrogen is the most common nutrient limiting primary production in freshwater, estuarine and coastal ecosystems. <i>M.homosphaera</i> possessed genes encoding nitrate, nitrite, and ammonium transporters, which indicates that <i>M.homosphaera</i> uses multiple forms of nitrogen (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">6</a>). Additionally, the <i>M.homosphaera</i> genome encoded one NAD(P)H-nitrate reductase, for the reduction of nitrate to nitrite, and one ferredoxin nitrite reductase, to catalyze the reduction of nitrite. Additionally, the <i>M.homosphaera</i> genome encoded all urea cycle components except for arginase, including carbamoyl-phosphate synthetase I (large and small subunits), ornithine carbamoyltransferase, arginosuccinate synthase, and arginosuccinate lyase.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec12">Microelements</h4><p><i>M.homosphaera</i> lacked all genes for common iron acquisition, although iron is involved in many metabolic activities, such as photosynthesis, respiration and nitrate reduction. Therefore, there may be a novel iron acquisition system in <i>M.homosphaera</i> that is different from those of other phytoplankton. The lack of iron transport components is also found in other picophytoplankton, such as <i>Ostreococcus</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Palenik B, Grimwood J, Aerts A, Rouze P, Salamov A, Putnam N, Dupont C, Jorgensen R, Derelle E, Rombauts S, et al. The tiny eukaryote Ostreococcus provides genomic insights into the paradox of plankton speciation. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2007;104(18):7705–10." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d18118e1933">25</a>], which also possesses a small genome size and gene number. Plants generally utilize copper transporter (CTR) family proteins to transport Cu ions into the cytosol [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Klaumann S, Nickolaus SD, Fuerst SH, Starck S, Schneider S, Neuhaus HE, Trentmann O. The tonoplast copper transporter COPT5 acts as an exporter and is required for interorgan allocation of copper in Arabidopsis thaliana. New Phytol. 2011;192(2):393–404." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d18118e1936">26</a>], and these proteins were not found in <i>M.homosphaera.</i> However, there were two ZIP (Zrt, Irt-like Protein) genes in this organism, which are speculated to function in Cu acquisition, perhaps showing the capacity to transport Cu<sup>2+</sup> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Pilon M. Moving copper in plants. New Phytol. 2011;192(2):305–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d18118e1945">27</a>].</p><p>In methionine synthesis, in addition to the VB<sub>12</sub> (vitamin B12)-dependent pathway, there are VB<sub>12</sub>-independent pathways in phytoplankton [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Helliwell KE, Wheeler GL, Leptos KC, Goldstein RE, Smith AG. Insights into the evolution of vitamin B-12 Auxotrophy from sequenced algal genomes. Mol Biol Evol. 2011;28(10):2921–33." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d18118e1955">28</a>]. However, <i>M.homosphaera</i> exhibited METH (methionine synthase) but not METE (B<sub>12</sub>-independent methionine synthase), indicating that it only performs methionine synthesis via the VB<sub>12</sub>-dependent pathway. Therefore, <i>M.homosphaera</i> shows strict dependence on VB<sub>12</sub>. VB<sub>12</sub> can only be produced by bacteria (both eubacteria and archaea) in nature [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Croft MT, Warren MJ, Smith AG. Algae need their vitamins. Eukaryot Cell. 2006;5(8):1175–83." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d18118e1973">29</a>]. Therefore, <i>M.homosphaera</i> must acquire VB<sub>12</sub> or a precursor from the lake environment or associated bacteria [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Croft MT, Lawrence AD, Raux-Deery E, Warren MJ, Smith AG. Algae acquire vitamin B-12 through a symbiotic relationship with bacteria. Nature. 2005;438(7064):90–3." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d18118e1982">30</a>]. In addition, the <i>M.homosphaera</i> genome possessed a complete pathway for thiamine biosynthesis (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">7</a>), which increases biotic and stress resistance [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Boubakri H, Gargouri M, Mliki A, Brini F, Chong J, Jbara M. Vitamins for enhancing plant resistance. Planta. 2016;244(3):529–43." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d18118e1991">31</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Rapala-Kozik M, Wolak N, Kujda M, Banas AK. The upregulation of thiamine (vitamin B-1) biosynthesis in Arabidopsis thaliana seedlings under salt and osmotic stress conditions is mediated by abscisic acid at the early stages of this stress response. BMC Plant Biol. 2012;12:2." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d18118e1994">32</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Bioenergetic metabolic pathway reconstruction in <i>M.homosphaera</i>
</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec14">Lipid metabolism</h4><p>Many microalgae, especially Sphaeropleales species, have been reported to produce considerable amounts of biofuels [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Suzuki S, Yamaguchi H, Nakajima N, Kawachi M. Raphidocelis subcapitata (=Pseudokirchneriella subcapitata) provides an insight into genome evolution and environmental adaptations in the Sphaeropleales. Sci Rep. 2018;8:8058." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2014">10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Bogen C, Al-Dilaimi A, Albersmeier A, Wichmann J, Grundmann M, Rupp O, Lauersen KJ, Blifernez-Klassen O, Kalinowski J, Goesmann A, et al. Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production. BMC Genomics. 2013;14:926." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2017">11</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Mandal S, Mallick N. Microalga Scenedesmus obliquus as a potential source for biodiesel production. Appl Microbiol Biotechnol. 2009;84(2):281–91." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2020">16</a>]. Therefore, lipid metabolism pathway reconstruction in <i>M.homosphaera</i> provides essential new insights into the lipid metabolism of phytoplankton and provides a basis for further investigations and genetic improvements. We compiled the genes related to lipid metabolism using the KEGG database to reconstruct the fatty acid biosynthesis pathways and TCA (glycerolipid) metabolism pathways of <i>M.homosphaera.</i> Additionally, we reconstructed the lipid metabolism pathways of other phytoplankton in the same way and compared them with those of <i>M.homosphaera</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig11">11</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">8</a>).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-11" data-title="Fig. 11"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig11" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 11</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/11" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig11_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-11-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig11_HTML.png" alt="figure11" loading="lazy" width="685" height="438" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-11-desc"><p>Fatty acid biosynthesis pathways (<b>a</b>) and the TCA (glycerolipid) metabolism pathways (<b>b</b>) of <i>Mychonastes homosphaera</i>. ACC: acetyl-CoA carboxylase, MAT: malonyl-CoA:ACP transacylase, KAS3: the beta-ketoacyl-acyl-carrier protein synthase 3, KAS1/2: the beta-ketoacyl-acyl-carrier-protein synthase, KAR: the 3-oxoacyl-ACP reductase, HAD: the beta-hydroxyacyl-ACP dehydrase, EAR: enoyl-ACP reductase, OAH: oleoyl-acyl-carrier-protein hydrolase, PAH: palmitoyl-protein thioesterase, GK: glycerol kinase, GPAT: glycerol-3phosphate O-acyltransferase, AGPAT: 1-acylglycerol-3phosphate O-acyltransferase, PP: phosphatidate phosphatase, DGAT: acyl-CoA:diacylglycerol acyltransferase, PDAT: phospholipid:diacylglycerol acyltransferase. Each coloured square represents a homologous gene, and different color represent different species. Pathway reconstruction was performed for fatty acid biosynthesis and TCA synthesis based on the KEGG database</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/11" data-track-dest="link:Figure11 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p><i>M.homosphaera</i> possesses a similar number of genes to other phytoplankton despite its small genome size, and the key fatty acid biosynthesis pathways have been identified. Notably, <i>M.homosphaera</i> exhibited a relatively high number of homologous genes for ACC (acetyl-CoA carboxylase, seven genes) and KAR (3-oxoacyl-ACP reductase, 11 genes). This situation also exists in other Sphaeropleales species.</p><p>Furthermore, the glycerolipid metabolism pathways of <i>M.homosphaera</i> were the same as those of other phytoplankton despite the different genome sizes. TAG (triacylglycerol) can be formed through glycerolipid metabolism via acylCoA–independent or dependent pathways catalyzed by PDAT (phospholipid: diacylglycerol acyltransferase) and DGAT (acyl-CoA:diacylglycerol acyltransferase), respectively. DGAT catalyzes the final step in the acylCoA–dependent pathway, leading to TAG. The DGAT gene family, including DGAT1 and DGAT2, was identified in the past decade [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Lardizabal KD, Mai JT, Wagner NW, Wyrick A, Voelker T, Hawkins DJ. DGAT2 is a new diacylglycerol acyltransferase gene family - purification, cloning, and expression in insect cells of two polypeptides from Mortierella ramanniana with diacylglycerol acyltransferase activity. J Biol Chem. 2001;276(42):38862–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2080">33</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Yen C-LE, Stone SJ, Koliwad S, Harris C, Farese RV Jr. DGAT enzymes and triacylglycerol biosynthesis. J Lipid Res. 2008;49(11):2283–301." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2083">34</a>]. DGAT2 was present in all the phytoplankton that we compared; however, DGAT1 only existed in <i>M.homosphaera</i> and other Sphaeropleales species. PDAT is a key enzyme in the acylCoA–independent pathway and is able to hydrolyze not only phospholipids, cholesteryl esters and galactolipids but also TAG. Therefore, PDAT plays an important role in membrane turnover as well as TAG synthesis and degradation. Although the total genome sizes of <i>M.homosphaera</i> and other picophytoplankton are significantly lower than those of other phytoplankton, the numbers of genes in glycerolipid metabolism pathways are not significantly different between these groups.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec15">Starch and isoprenoid metabolism</h4><p>We analyzed genes related to starch metabolism using the KEGG database and Busi’s research [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Busi MV, Barchiesi J, Martin M, Gomez-Casati DF. Starch metabolism in green algae. Starch-Starke. 2014;66(1–2):28–40." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2100">35</a>] to reconstruct the starch metabolism pathways of <i>M.homosphaera</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig12">12</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">9</a>). There are four biochemical steps in starch synthesis: substrate activation, chain elongation, chain branching, and chain debranching [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Zeeman SC, Kossmann J, Smith AM. Starch: Its Metabolism, Evolution, and Biotechnological Modification in Plants. In: Merchant S, Briggs WR, Ort D, editors. Annual Review of Plant Biology, Vol 61; 2010. p. 209–34." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2112">36</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Preiss J, Ball K, Smithwhite B, Iglesias A, Kakefuda G, Li L. Starch biosynthesis and its regulation. Biochem Soc Trans. 1991;19(3):539–47." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2116">37</a>].
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-12" data-title="Fig. 12"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig12" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 12</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/12" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig12_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-12-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig12_HTML.png" alt="figure12" loading="lazy" width="685" height="343" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-12-desc"><p>Starch synthesis (<b>a</b>) and degradation (<b>b</b>) in <i>Mychonastes homosphaera</i>. glgC: glucose-1-phosphate adenylyltransferase, WAXY: granule-bound starch synthase, glgA: starch synthase, glgB: glucan branching enzyme, ISA: isoamylase, R1: alpha-glucan, water dikinase, PWD: phosphoglucan, water dikinase, amyA: alpha-amylase, amyB: beta-amylase, malQ: 4-alpha-glucanotransferase, glgP: starch phosphorylase. Pathway reconstruction was performed for starch synthesis and starch degradation based on the KEGG database</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/12" data-track-dest="link:Figure12 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>There are two isoprenoid biosynthesis pathways found in organisms: the mevalonate (MVA) and the nonmevalonate (DXP) pathways [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Eisenreich W, Bacher A, Arigoni D, Rohdich F. Biosynthesis of isoprenoids via the non-mevalonate pathway. Cell Mol Life Sci. 2004;61(12):1401–26." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2149">38</a>]. Similar to other green algae, such as <i>C.reinhardtii, Scenedesmus obliquus</i> and <i>Ostreococcus lucimarinus</i>, <i>M.homosphaera</i> has abandoned the MVA pathway and retained only the DXP pathway (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">10</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec16" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec16-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec16">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec16-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Environmental prevalence and adaptation of <i>M.homosphaera</i>
</h3><p>Understanding the response of this algae to a stressful and fluctuating environment offers the promise of advancing both applied and fundamental research. <i>Mychonastes</i> is a common chlorophycean picoplankton genus in freshwater ecosystems [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Krienitz L, Bock C, Dadheech PK, Proeschold T. Taxonomic reassessment of the genus Mychonastes (Chlorophyceae, Chlorophyta) including the description of eight new species. Phycologia. 2011;50(1):89–106." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2185">39</a>] and is widely found in lakes and rivers in Asia [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Shi X, Li S, Fan F, Zhang M, Yang Z, Yang Y. Mychonastes dominates the photosynthetic picoeukaryotes in Lake Poyang, a river-connected lake. FEMS Microbiol Ecol. 2019;95(1):fiy211." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2188">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li S, Bronner G, Lepere C, Kong F, Shi X.
