Temporal and spatial variations in the composition of freshwater photosynthetic picoeukaryotes revealed by MiSeq sequencing from flow cytometry sorted samples. Environ Microbiol. 2017;19(6):2286–300." href="#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2191">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hanagata N, Malinsky-Rushansky N, Dubinsky Z. Eukaryotic picoplankton, Mychonastes homosphaera (Chlorophyceae, Chlorophyta), in Lake Kinneret, Israel. Phycol Res. 2010;47(4):263–9." href="#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2191_1">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Wu L, Xu L, Hu C. Screening and characterization of oleaginous microalgal species from northern Xinjiang. J Microbiol Biotechnol. 2015;25(6):910–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2194">42</a>], Europe [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Hepperle D, Schlegel I. Molecular diversity of eucaryotic picoalgae from three lakes in Switzerland. Int Rev Hydrobiol. 2002;87(1):1–10." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2198">43</a>] and North America [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Phillips KA, Fawley MW. Diversity of coccoid algae in shallow lakes during winter. Phycologia. 2000;39(6):498–506." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2201">44</a>]. <i>Mychonastes</i> species are small (&lt; 3 μm) unicellular organisms (spherical, ovate and ellipsoid) surrounded by a cell wall, possessing one mitochondria and chloroplast. <i>Mychonastes</i> species are prevalent in river-connected lakes where diatoms occur [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Reynolds CS, Descy JP, Padisak J. Are phytoplankton dynamics in rivers so different from those in shallow lakes? Hydrobiologia. 1994;289(1–3):1–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2210">45</a>], and recent research has shown that <i>Mychonastes</i> is the dominant eukaryotic picophytoplankton group in spring and winter in many eutrophic lakes, such as Lake Chaohu and Lake Poyang [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Li S, Shi X, Lepère C, Liu M, Wang X, Kong F. Unexpected predominance of photosynthetic picoeukaryotes in shallow eutrophic lakes. J Plankton Res. 2016;38(4):830–42." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2217">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Shi X, Li S, Fan F, Zhang M, Yang Z, Yang Y. Mychonastes dominates the photosynthetic picoeukaryotes in Lake Poyang, a river-connected lake. FEMS Microbiol Ecol. 2019;95(1):fiy211." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2220">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Li S, Bronner G, Lepere C, Kong F, Shi X. Temporal and spatial variations in the composition of freshwater photosynthetic picoeukaryotes revealed by MiSeq sequencing from flow cytometry sorted samples. Environ Microbiol. 2017;19(6):2286–300." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2223">40</a>]. The whole genome of <i>M.homosphaera</i> could provide insight into its adaptive mechanism.</p><p>For phytoplankton, only a few resources are potentially limiting, e.g., light, nitrogen, phosphorus, inorganic carbon, silicon, iron,, and sometimes a few trace metals or vitamins [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Huisman J, Weissing FJ. Biodiversity of plankton by species oscillations and chaos. Nature. 1999;402(6760):407–10." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2232">46</a>]. <i>M.homosphaera</i> possesses many nutrient transport genes, such as genes encoding nitrogen, phosphorus, metal, and vitamin transporters, that may improve its nutrient uptake ability at a relatively low trophic level. In addition, <i>M.homosphaera</i> could use various sources of nitrogen compounds, strengthening its competitive ability under nutrient-depleted conditions. The presence of various potential carbon-concentrating mechanisms in <i>M.homosphaera</i> would provide more possibilities for carbon assimilation under different environmental conditions. Furthermore, for the supplementation of micronutrient such as metals and vitamins, <i>M.homosphaera</i> possesses its own acquisition pathway.</p><p>Light intensity is relatively low and becomes an important limiting factor for phytoplankton growth in spring and winter. <i>M.homosphaera</i> possesses gene homologs of phototropins, cryptochromes and phytochromes, which absorb red light and blue light [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Hegemann P. Algal sensory photoreceptors. Annu Rev Plant Biol. 2008;59:167–89." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2253">47</a>]. Compared with its major counterpart taxon, the diatoms, <i>M.homosphaera</i> lacks rhodopsins, which absorb green light. Red light and blue light are both absorbed at relatively shallow depths, but green light usually penetrates the water column to greater depths than other wavelengths [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Hamilton DP, Collier KJ, Quinn JM, Howard-Williams C. Lake restoration handbook: a New Zealand perspective. Cham: Springer; 2018." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2259">48</a>]. Thus, the presence of blue/red-light photoreceptors and the absence of green-light photoreceptors increases the adaptation of <i>M.homosphaera</i> to the shallow turbid lake and implies genome streamlining. In addition, the high abundance of light harvesting complex components in <i>M.homosphaera</i> contributes to its adaptation to the low light conditions found in Lake Chaohu in winter.</p><p>Light is essential for algae, but excess light will damage the growth of algae. Phytoplankton adjust their light absorption and eliminate excess light energy when light is abundant or excessive [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Li Z, Wakao S, Fischer BB, Niyogi KK. Sensing and responding to excess light. Annu Rev Plant Biol. 2009;60:239–60." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2272">49</a>]. <i>M.homosphaera</i> possesses the light dissipation mechanism of the xanthophyll cycle, which provides the ability to remove accumulated harmful byproducts, such as three line states of chlorophyll molecules and singlet oxygen, resulting from excess light [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Walters RG. Towards an understanding of photosynthetic acclimation. J Exp Bot. 2005;56(411):435–47." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2278">50</a>]. In addition, the existence of Fe-Mn SODs will decrease the damage caused by reactive oxygen generated by excess light [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Mittler R. Oxidative stress, antioxidants and stress tolerance. Trends Plant Sci. 2002;7(9):405–10." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2281">51</a>].</p><p>The water temperatures of Lake Chaohu in winter are normally lower than 10 °C [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Li S, Bronner G, Lepere C, Kong F, Shi X. Temporal and spatial variations in the composition of freshwater photosynthetic picoeukaryotes revealed by MiSeq sequencing from flow cytometry sorted samples. Environ Microbiol. 2017;19(6):2286–300." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2288">40</a>], which is a challenge for phytoplankton growth. To adjust to the winter conditions in the lake, <i>M.homosphaera</i> possesses many mechanisms to respond to low temperatures. <i>M.homosphaera</i> possesses a gene encoding a pyrroline-5-carboxylate reductase that can synthesize proline to alleviate osmotic stress caused by cold. <i>M.homosphaera</i> also possesses genes encoding antioxidases (such as Fe-Mn SOD, catalase, and ascorbate peroxidase) and antioxidant (such as glutamate and β-carotene) biosynthetic pathways. Both of these substances can remove toxic active oxygen derived from excess O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> in cells under cold stress [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Van Breusegem F, Slooten L, Stassart JM, Botterman J, Moens T, Van Montagu M, Inze D. Effects of overproduction of tobacco MnSOD in maize chloroplasts on foliar tolerance to cold and oxidative stress. J Exp Bot. 1999;50(330):71–8." href="#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2307">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fryer MJ, Andrews JR, Oxborough K, Blowers DA, Baker NR. Relationship between CO2 assimilation, photosynthetic electron transport, and active O-2 metabolism in leaves of maize in the field during periods of low temperature. Plant Physiol. 1998;116(2):571–80." href="#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2307_1">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Prasad TK. Role of catalase in inducing chilling tolerance in pre-emergent maize seedlings. Plant Physiol. 1997;114(4):1369–76." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2310">54</a>]. Unsaturated fatty acids can reduce the phase transition temperature of the plant cytomembrane to prevent plants from being damaged by temperature stress [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lyons JM. Chilling injury in plants. Annu Rev Plant Physiol Plant Mol Biol. 1973;24:445–66." href="#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2313">55</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Murata N, Los DA. Membrane fluidity and temperature perception. Plant Physiol. 1997;115(3):875–9." href="#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2313_1">56</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nishida I, Murata N. Chilling sensitivity in plants and cyanobacteria: the crucial contribution of membrane lipids. Annu Rev Plant Physiol Plant Mol Biol. 1996;47:541–68." href="#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2313_2">57</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Somerville C. Direct tests of the role of membrane lipid composition in low-temperature-induced photoinhibition and chilling sensitivity in plants and cyanobacteria. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 1995;92(14):6215–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2316">58</a>]. <i>M.homosphaera</i> possesses genes encoding the synthesis of large fatty acids, especially unsaturated fatty acids. The genome sequence of <i>M.homosphaera</i> indicates enrichment of unsaturated fatty acids, which is consistent with other research (more than 70%) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Yuan C, Liu J, Fan Y, Ren X, Hu G, Li F. Mychonastes afer HSO-3-1 as a potential new source of biodiesel. Biotechnol Biofuels. 2011;4:47. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/1754-6834-1184-1147&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2326">59</a>]. The high content of unsaturated fatty acids reduces the <i>M.homosphaera</i> phase transition temperature and improves the adaptation of this species in Lake Chaohu. In addition, phytochromes act as temperature sensors that integrate temperature information over the course of the night [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Jung JH, Domijan M, Klose C, Biswas S, Ezer D, Gao MJ, Khattak AK, Box MS, Charoensawan V, Cortijo S, et al. Phytochromes function as thermosensors in Arabidopsis. Science. 2016;354(6314):886–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2332">60</a>], as warm temperatures reduce the activation of phytochromes, which may improve the adaptation of <i>M.homosphaera</i> to temperature differences between day and night.</p><p>Copper deficiency can lead to the inhibition of organism growth, photosynthesis, and respiration. However, excessive copper also has obvious toxic effects on algal bodies, even leading to plant death [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Fawaz EG, Salam DA, Kamareddine L. Evaluation of copper toxicity using site specific algae and water chemistry: field validation of laboratory bioassays. Ecotoxicol Environ Saf. 2018;155:59–65." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2341">61</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Wang H, Ebenezer V, Ki J-S. Photosynthetic and biochemical responses of the freshwater green algae Closterium ehrenbergii Meneghini (Conjugatophyceae) exposed to the metal coppers and its implication for toxicity testing. J Microbiol. 2018;56(6):426–34." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2344">62</a>]. To adapt to an excessive copper environment, <i>M.homosphaera</i> may possess multiple mechanisms for ameliorating copper toxicity. <i>M.homosphaera</i> encodes a phytochelatin synthase that can chelate metal ions to reduce copper toxicity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Ahner BA, Morel FMM. Phytochelatin production in marine algae. 2. Induction by various metals. Limnol Oceanogr. 1995;40(4):658–65." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2353">63</a>] and a Fe-Mn SOD to dispel the free radicals formed due to copper stress [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Alscher RG, Erturk N, Heath LS. Role of superoxide dismutases (SODs) in controlling oxidative stress in plants. J Exp Bot. 2002;53(372):1331–41." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2357">64</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">The <i>M.homosphaera</i> genome reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production</h3><p>Algae are also very important because they synthesize a number of different lipids and carbon storage compounds (such as starch and isoprenoid) that possess high biological and commercial value [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Busi MV, Barchiesi J, Martin M, Gomez-Casati DF. Starch metabolism in green algae. Starch-Starke. 2014;66(1–2):28–40." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2372">35</a>]. These characteristics, including high biomass production rates, high starch content and TAG accumulation, make algae a possible biofuel resource. Compared with starch accumulation, Sphaeropleales may show an advantage in TAG accumulation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Bogen C, Al-Dilaimi A, Albersmeier A, Wichmann J, Grundmann M, Rupp O, Lauersen KJ, Blifernez-Klassen O, Kalinowski J, Goesmann A, et al. Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production. BMC Genomics. 2013;14:926." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2375">11</a>].</p><p>The complete lipid pathways and abundant homologous gene family in the <i>M.homosphaera</i> genome indicate a high potential lipid yield. Furthermore, the quality of biodiesel is mainly determined by the composition of fatty acids [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Mukta N, Murthy IYLN, Sripal P. Variability assessment in Pongamia pinnata (L.) Pierre germplasm for biodiesel traits. Ind Crop Prod. 2009;29(2–3):536–40." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2384">65</a>]. The abundance of KCS (3-ketoacyl-CoA synthase) and FAD (fatty acid desaturase) homologous genes indicates that <i>M.homosphaera</i> tends to synthesize long-chain unsaturated fatty acids, which are generally well suited for biodiesel generation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Ramos MJ, Fernandez CM, Casas A, Rodriguez L, Perez A. Influence of fatty acid composition of raw materials on biodiesel properties. Bioresour Technol. 2009;100(1):261–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2390">66</a>]. In addition, TAG accumulation in phytoplankton usually occurs under stress conditions such as high light or nitrogen starvation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Hu Q, Sommerfeld M, Jarvis E, Ghirardi M, Posewitz M, Seibert M, Darzins A. Microalgal triacylglycerols as feedstocks for biofuel production: perspectives and advances. Plant J. 2008;54(4):621–39." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2393">67</a>]. The TAG yield can also be increased artificially by inhibition of starch synthesis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Li Y, Han D, Hu G, Sommerfeld M, Hu Q. Inhibition of starch synthesis results in overproduction of lipids in Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. Biotechnol Bioeng. 2010;107(2):258–68." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2397">68</a>]. Laboratory experiments revealed that <i>M.homosphaera</i> could have a high growth rate even under environmental stress [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Liu C, Shi X, Fan F, Wu F, Lei J. N:P ratio influences the competition of Microcystis with its picophytoplankton counterparts, Mychonastes and Synechococcus, under nutrient enrichment conditions. J Freshwat Ecol. 2019;34(1):445–54." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2403">69</a>]. In addition to the high specific uptake rate of CO<sub>2</sub>, <i>M.homosphaera</i> is capable of utilizing polysaccharides as a carbon source. Therefore, extensive carbon sources, efficient photosynthesis, rapid growth and lipid abundance indicate that <i>M.homosphaera</i> could be a preferred source for biodiesel production. Environmental condition control and biotechnological applications to improve the yield and quality of TAG would make <i>M.homosphaera</i> more appropriate to biodiesel production.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Genome streamlining of <i>M.homosphaera</i>
</h3><p>It is commonly believed that evolution generally proceeds towards increased complexity at both the genomic and organismal levels. However, recent evolutionary reconstructions indicate that genome reduction is a dominant mode of genome evolution, consistent with the optimization of initially highly complex large genomes, leading to the acquisition of adaptive innovations [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Wolf YI, Koonin EV. Genome reduction as the dominant mode of evolution. Bioessays. 2013;35(9):829–37." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2430">70</a>]. <i>M.homosphaera</i> and other picophytoplankton, such as <i>Ostreococcus tauri</i> and <i>Micromonas commoda,</i> possess smaller and more streamlined genomes than phytoplankton of the same genus. Streamlined genomes are characteristic of fast-evolving species that live in specialized ecological niches or in extreme environments [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Giovannoni SJ, Thrash JC, Temperton B. Implications of streamlining theory for microbial ecology. ISME J. 2014;8(8):1553–65." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2442">71</a>]. The whole-genome analysis suggested that there are two routes for genome reduction in <i>M.homosphaera</i>, namely, the reduction of gene family number and of noncoding region (intergenic and intron) size. Although <i>M.homosphaera</i> possesses the minimum number of gene families in the order Sphaeropleales, this species has retained all the fundamental functional genes and can grow well in uni-algal laboratory experiments [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Liu C, Shi X, Fan F, Wu F, Lei J. N:P ratio influences the competition of Microcystis with its picophytoplankton counterparts, Mychonastes and Synechococcus, under nutrient enrichment conditions. J Freshwat Ecol. 2019;34(1):445–54." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2452">69</a>]. In addition, <i>M.homosphaera</i> possesses nearly the most dense nuclear genome (24.23 Mb) in Chlorophyceae, second only to <i>R</i>.<i>subcapitata</i>, with mean intergenic and intron lengths of 685 and 359 bp, respectively. This streamlining is particularly obvious in organelle genomes, including those of chloroplasts and mitochondria. <i>M.homosphaera</i> have the highest protein coding density among Sphaeropleales<i>,</i> and these genomes do not contain introns, leading to the reduction in organelle genomes. This compact nuclear genome has also been found in other picophytoplankton, such as <i>O.tauri</i> and <i>M.commoda</i>, although their genetic relationships are relatively distant. This result may indicate that the genome architecture is consistent with cell size in phytoplankton, although the relationship between the genome architecture and cell size remains controversial [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lynch M, Conery JS. The origins of genome complexity. Science. 2003;302(5649):1401–4." href="#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2477">72</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lynch M. Streamlining and simplification of microbial genome architecture. In: Annu Rev Microbiol vol 60; 2006. p. 327–49." href="#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2477_1">73</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Smith DR, Hamaji T, Olson BJSC, Durand PM, Ferris P, Michod RE, Featherston J, Nozaki H, Keeling PJ. Organelle genome complexity scales positively with organism size in Volvocine Green algae. Mol Biol Evol. 2013;30(4):793–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2480">74</a>]. Such streamlined genomes are also detectable in the cyanobacterium <i>Prochlorococcus sp.</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Partensky F, Garczarek L. Prochlorococcus: advantages and limits of minimalism. Annu Rev Mar Sci. 2010;2:305–31." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2487">75</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Dufresne A, Garczarek L, Partensky F. Accelerated evolution associated with genome reduction in a free-living prokaryote. Genome Biol. 2005:6(2):R14." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2490">76</a>] and the alpha-proteobacterium <i>Candidatus Pelagibacter ubique</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Morris JJ, Lenski RE, Zinser ER. The Black Queen Hypothesis: Evolution of Dependencies through Adaptive Gene Loss. Mbio. 2012;3(2):e00036–12." href="#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2496">77</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Giovannoni SJ, Tripp HJ, Givan S, Podar M, Vergin KL, Baptista D, Bibbs L, Eads J, Richardson TH, Noordewier M, et al. Genome streamlining in a cosmopolitan oceanic bacterium. Science. 2005;309(5738):1242–5." href="#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2496_1">78</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Viklund J, Ettema TJG, Andersson SGE. Independent genome reduction and phylogenetic reclassification of the oceanic SAR11 clade. Mol Biol Evol. 2012;29(2):599–615." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2499">79</a>], both of which are highly successful free-living organisms and apparently the most abundant cellular life forms on earth.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20"><i>M.homosphaera</i> genomes provide insights into evolutionary processes</h3><p>Previous research indicates that the algae in Sphaeropleales harbor split cox2 genes, including a mitochondrion-localized cox2a gene and a nucleus-localized cox2b gene [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Rodriguez-Salinas E, Riveros-Rosas H, Li Z, Fucikova K, Brand JJ, Lewis LA, Gonzalez-Halphen D. Lineage-specific fragmentation and nuclear relocation of the mitochondrial cox2 gene in chlorophycean green algae (Chlorophyta). Mol Phylogenet Evol. 2012;64(1):166–76." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2513">80</a>]. Consistent with this finding, <i>M.homosphaera</i> harbored split cox2 genes, comprising a mitochondrion-localized cox2a gene and a nucleus-localized cox2b gene. However, Prasinophyceae, Ulvophyceae and Trebouxiophyceae contain orthodox, intact, mitochondrial cox2 genes, having evolved earlier than Chlamydomonadales, which possess both split cox2 genes in their nuclear genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Perez-Martinez X, Antaramian A, Vazquez-Acevedo M, Funes S, Tolkunova E, d'Alayer J, Claros MG, Davidson E, King MP, Gonzalez-Halphen D. Subunit II of cytochrome c oxidase in chlamydomonad algae is a heterodimer encoded by two independent nuclear genes. J Biol Chem. 2001;276(14):11302–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2519">81</a>]. Sphaeropleales show an intermediate trait of gene migration to the nucleus, which indicates that the appearance of Sphaeropleales occurred between that of Ulvophyceae and Chlamydomonadales.</p><p>In addition, the presence of NUMTs (nuclear mitochondrial DNA) and NUPTs (nuclear plastid DNA) (Supplementary Text, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">12</a>) in <i>M.homosphaera</i> genomes verified gene migration from organelles to the nucleus. This is an important process for genome streamlining in most eukaryotes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Blanchard JL, Schmidt GW. Pervasive migration of organellar DNA to the nucleus in plants. J Mol Evol. 1995;41(4):397–406." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2534">82</a>], as migrated genes are usually inserted into the intergenic space of the nuclear genome to improve the coding rate of the nuclear genome. During the evolution of mitochondria, many mitochondrial genes have been functionally transferred to the nucleus, whereas others have been replaced by preexisting nuclear genes which possessed similar function [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Adams KL, Palmer JD. Evolution of mitochondrial gene content: gene loss and transfer to the nucleus. Mol Phylogenet Evol. 2003;29(3):380–95." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2537">83</a>].</p><p>The Endosymbiotic Hypothesis is a hypothesis about the origins of mitochondria and chloroplasts, which are organelles of eukaryotic cells. According to this, land plants and green algae acquired their plastids from the same endosymbiotic event, the Glaucophyta and red algae (Rhodophyta), likely also originated from secondary endosymbiosis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Rodriguez-Ezpeleta N, Brinkmann H, Burey SC, Roure B, Burger G, Loffelhardt W, Bohnert HJ, Philippe H, Lang BF. Monophyly of primary photosynthetic eukaryotes: Green plants, red algae, and glaucophytes. Curr Biol. 2005;15(14):1325–30." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2543">84</a>]. The DXP pathway is provided by the eukaryotic host cell during the primary endosymbiotic event in phototrophs with primary plastids. In contrast, the MVA pathway is contributed by the secondary eukaryotic host, with possible contributions from the primary host cell in algal groups emerging from secondary endosymbiosis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Lohr M, Schwender J, Polle JEW. Isoprenoid biosynthesis in eukaryotic phototrophs: a spotlight on algae. Plant Sci. 2012;185:9–22." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2546">85</a>]. Similar to other green algae, such as <i>C.reinhardtii, S.obliquus</i> and <i>O.lucimarinus</i>, <i>M.homosphaera</i> had abandoned the MVA pathway and retained only the DXP pathway [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Radakovits R, Jinkerson RE, Fuerstenberg SI, Tae H, Settlage RE, Boore JL, Posewitz MC. Draft genome sequence and genetic transformation of the oleaginous alga Nannochloropis gaditana. Nat Commun. 2012;3:10." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2559">24</a>]. However, the primary endosymbiotic algal groups Glaucophyta and Rhodophyta (except <i>C.merolae</i>) and secondary endosymbiotic algal groups Euglenophyta, Chlorarachniophyta, Haptophyta and Heterokontophyta have maintained both the MVA and DXP pathways. Therefore, the abandonment of the MVA pathway and the presence of the DXP pathway in <i>M.homosphaera</i> could support the endosymbiosis hypothesis.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec21" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec21-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec21">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec21-content"><p>This study focused on the analysis of the whole genome of <i>M.homosphaera</i>, a eukaryotic picophytoplankton, providing insights into the environmental adaptation mechanism of these organisms, including efficient and comprehensive nutrient utilization capacity, a special light harvesting system to adapt to low light conditions, a multifarious defense mechanism against coldness, and various response mechanisms to improve resistance to environmental stress. In addition, <i>M.homosphaera</i> exhibits high lipid yields, particularly of long-chain unsaturated fatty acids. Therefore, this species is generally well suited for biodiesel generation. Similar to other picophytoplankton, streamlining and genome compaction was observed for <i>M.homosphaera</i> Streamlining of the genome of <i>M.homosphaera</i> may be caused by the reduction in gene family number and noncoding region size. With respect to genetic evolution, the split cox2 genes in <i>M.homosphaera</i> indicate gene migration from the organelles to the nucleus and the abandonment of the MVA pathway, and the presence of the DXP pathway in <i>M.homosphaera</i> could support the endosymbiosis hypothesis.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec22" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec22-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec22">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec22-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Isolation and culture of <i>M.homosphaera</i>
</h3><p><i>M.homosphaera</i> was isolated from the central region Lake Chaohu (31.5294°N, 117.5261°E) in December 2015, and preserved in our laboratory at the Nanjing Institute of Geography and Limnology, Chinese Academy of Sciences. The strain was cultivated at 15 °C in BG11 medium under light (12 h light:12 h dark; 25 μmol photo m<sup>− 2</sup> s<sup>− 1</sup>) in 250-mL glass flasks.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">DNA extraction</h3><p><i>M.homosphaera</i> was collected, and genomic DNA was extracted by using the QIAGEN Genomic DNA Extraction Kit (Cat# 13323, QIAGEN) according to the standard operating procedure provided by the manufacturer. The extracted DNA was detected by a NanoDrop™ One UV-Vis spectrophotometer (Thermo Fisher Scientific, USA) for DNA purity (OD260/280 ranging from 1.8 to 2.0 and OD 260/230 is between 2.0 and 2.2), and then, a Qubit 3.0 fluorometer (Invitrogen, USA) was used to quantify the DNA accurately.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Library construction and sequencing</h3><p>After the sample was qualitatively analyzed, the genomic DNA was sheared by using g-TUBEs (Covaris, USA) according to the expected sizes of the fragments for the library. The fragmented DNA of the target size was enriched and purified by using MegBeads. Next, the fragmented DNA was repaired for damage and then end-repaired. The stem-loop adaptor was linked on both ends of each DNA fragment, and the link-failed fragments were removed by exonuclease. Then, the target fragments were screened by BluePippin (Sage Science, USA) and purified to construct the library. Finally, an Agilent 2100 Bioanalyzer (Agilent Technologies, USA) was used to determine the sizes of the library fragments.</p><p>After the library was constructed, DNA templates and enzyme complexes of a certain concentration and volume were transferred to the ZMWs of the Sequel system (Pacific Biosciences, USA) for real-time single-molecule sequencing.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Assembly and annotation</h3><p>A total of 5.8 G high-quality subreads were generated with a mean length of 6.6 kb and subreads N50 9.8 kb (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig13">13</a>). For genome assembly, the subreads were first rectified with canu [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Koren S, Walenz BP, Berlin K, Miller JR, Bergman NH, Phillippy AM. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 2017;27(5):722–36." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2650">86</a>] to obtain trimmed reads, and Wtdbg (<a href="https://github.com/ruanjue/wtdbg">https://github.com/ruanjue/wtdbg</a>) was used to perform assembly based on these trimmed reads. The draft assembly contigs were then corrected using Pacbio reads with Quiver [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Chin C-S, Alexander DH, Marks P, Klammer AA, Drake J, Heiner C, Clum A, Copeland A, Huddleston J, Eichler EE, et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat Methods. 2013;10(6):563." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2660">87</a>]. Then, the quivered contigs were further polished with Illumina reads by using Pilon [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Walker BJ, Abeel T, Shea T, Priest M, Abouelliel A, Sakthikumar S, Cuomo CA, Zeng Q, Wortman J, Young SK, et al. Pilon: An Integrated Tool for Comprehensive Microbial Variant Detection and Genome Assembly Improvement. PLoS One. 2014;9(11):e112963." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2663">88</a>]. Finally, the corrected genomic sequence was aligned to the nt database (Nucleotide Sequence Database, <a href="ftp://ftp.ncbi.nih.gov/blast/db">ftp://ftp.ncbi.nih.gov/blast/db</a>), and the relevant plant, algae and no-hit sequences were retained. The final assembly genome was 24.23 Mb with contig N50 2 Mb, contig number 31. Length of the longest sequence is 2,973,032 bp, and 12 contigs make up for 90% of the total genome. BUSCO [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Simao FA, Waterhouse RM, Ioannidis P, Kriventseva EV, Zdobnov EM. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics. 2015;31(19):3210–2." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2674">89</a>] analysis showed that 89.4% of the 303 core genes in the eukaryote dataset were complete (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">1</a>), which indicated that most highly conserved genomes were well packed and that the assembly results were reliable.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-13" data-title="Fig. 13"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig13" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 13</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/13" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig13_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-13-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-020-06891-6/MediaObjects/12864_2020_6891_Fig13_HTML.png" alt="figure13" loading="lazy" width="685" height="413" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-13-desc"><p>Histogram with raw read length vs. number of raw reads</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6/figures/13" data-track-dest="link:Figure13 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The interspersed repetitive sequences were predicted using MISA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Thiel T, Michalek W, Varshney RK, Graner A. Exploiting EST databases for the development and characterization of gene-derived SSR-markers in barley (Hordeum vulgare L.). Theor Appl Genet. 2003;106(3):411–22." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2700">90</a>]. Two strategies were adopted to annotate repetitive sequences: RepeatMask based on aligning with a database or RepeatModeler [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Bedell JA, Korf I, Gish W. MaskerAid: a performance enhancement to RepeatMasker. Bioinformatics. 2000;16(11):1040–1." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2703">91</a>] based on constructing a de novo repeat database. For gene structure annotation, the de novo prediction method Augustus [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Stanke M, Waack S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics. 2003;19:II215–25." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2706">92</a>]; homology alignment prediction, such as with GeneWise [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Birney E, Durbin R. Using GeneWise in the Drosophila annotation experiment. Genome Res. 2000;10(4):547–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2709">93</a>] and EST/cDNA; and genome alignment prediction approach, including PASA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Haas BJ, Salzberg SL, Zhu W, Pertea M, Allen JE, Orvis J, White O, Buell CR, Wortman JR. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 2008;9(1):R7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2712">94</a>], were used separately and integrated through EVM [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Haas BJ, Salzberg SL, Zhu W, Pertea M, Allen JE, Orvis J, White O, Buell CR, Wortman JR. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 2008;9(1):R7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2716">94</a>]. Transposon PSI [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Yagi M, Kosugi S, Hirakawa H, Ohmiya A, Tanase K, Harada T, Kishimoto K, Nakayama M, Ichimura K, Onozaki T, et al. Sequence analysis of the genome of carnation (Dianthus caryophyllus L.). DNA Res. 2014;21(3):231–41." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2719">95</a>] alignment was performed to remove genes containing transposable elements.</p><p>The predicted protein sequences were searched against KEGG, COG (Cluster of Orthologous Groups), NR (Non-Redundant Protein Database), Trembl and Swissprot to predict gene functions and metabolic information through Blastall [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Haas BJ, Salzberg SL, Zhu W, Pertea M, Allen JE, Orvis J, White O, Buell CR, Wortman JR. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 2008;9(1):R7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2725">94</a>]. A whole-genome blast was performed against the secondary database InterPro to predict conserved sequences and domains of proteins using InterProScan [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Zdobnov EM, Apweiler R. InterProScan - an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics. 2001;17(9):847–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2728">96</a>]. For noncoding RNA annotation, both strategies, including alignment with the existing noncoding RNA database Rfam [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Griffiths-Jones S, Moxon S, Marshall M, Khanna A, Eddy SR, Bateman A. Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2005;33:D121–4." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2731">97</a>] and predictions with tRNAscan-SE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Lowe TM, Eddy SR. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 1997;25(5):955–64." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2734">98</a>] or RNAmmer [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Lagesen K, Hallin P, Rodland EA, Staerfeldt H-H, Rognes T, Ussery DW. RNAmmer: consistent and rapid annotation of ribosomal RNA genes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007;35(9):3100–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2737">99</a>], were adopted. The major software program versions and parameter settings has been described in the Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">13</a>.</p><p>This Whole Genome Shotgun project has been deposited at DDBJ/ENA/GenBank under the accession VXJC00000000. The version described in this paper is version VXJC01000000. Gene annotation file and the gene set files have been provided as supplementary files (Supplementary file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">3</a>). And the PacBio data has been deposited at NCBI under BioProject number PRJNA556117.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>We investigated the phylogenetic position of <i>M.homosphaera</i> by using 18S rDNA, which is a widely used molecular barcode for taxonomic affiliation in phytoplankton [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Piganeau G, Eyre-Walker A, Grimsley N, Moreau H. How and why DNA barcodes underestimate the diversity of microbial eukaryotes. PLoS One. 2011;6(2):6." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2768">100</a>]. The analysis involved 18 nucleotide sequences from NCBI (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">14</a>), and all positions containing gaps and missing data were eliminated. There were a total of 1765 positions in the final data set. Maximum likelihood (ML) analysis was conducted based on the Tamura-Nei model [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Tamura K, Nei M. Estimation of the number of nucleotide substitutions in the control region of mitochondrial DNA in humans and chimpanzees. Mol Biol Evol. 1993;10(3):512–26." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2774">101</a>] in MEGA X [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Kumar S, Stecher G, Li M, Knyaz C, Tamura K. MEGA X: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis across computing platforms. Mol Biol Evol. 2018;35(6):1547–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d18118e2777">102</a>], with 1000 bootstrap repetitions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">Drawing tool</h3><p>OrganellarGenomeDRAW 1.3.1 was used to generate Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig3">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#Fig4">4</a>. and Adobe Illustrator CS6 was used to generate other figures.</p></div></div></section>
                    <section aria-labelledby="availability-of-data-and-materials" data-title="Availability of data and materials"><div class="c-article-section" id="availability-of-data-and-materials-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="availability-of-data-and-materials">Availability of data and materials</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="availability-of-data-and-materials-content">
              
              <p>The datasets generated during the current study are available in the NCBI repository under the accession VXJC00000000. And accession numbers of the reference datasets such as the nucleotide sequences and genomic sequences form NCBI repository had been listed in the Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">14</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-020-06891-6#MOESM1">15</a>.</p>
